  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_47641db1-a25d-4122-9651-17d52601832a'
      });
    }

They have a beautiful new baby son named Julian, and their close friend Ferm√≠n Romero de Torres is about to be wed. But their joy is eclipsed when a mysterious stranger visits the Sempere bookshop and threatens to divulge a terrible secret that has been buried for two decades in t</span>
  <span id="freeText6716120434051691339" style="display:none">Barcelona, 1957. It is Christmas, and Daniel Sempere and his wife Bea have much to celebrate. They have a beautiful new baby son named Julian, and their close friend Ferm√≠n Romero de Torres is about to be wed. But their joy is eclipsed when a mysterious stranger visits the Sempere bookshop and threatens to divulge a terrible secret that has been buried for two decades in the city's dark past. His appearance plunges Ferm√≠n and Daniel into a dangerous adventure that will take them back to the 1940's and the dark early days of Franco's dictatorship. The terrifying events of that time launch them on a journey fraught with jealousy, suspicion, vengeance, and lies, a search for the truth that will put into peril everything they love and ultimately transform their lives.<br /> <br />Full of intrigue and emotion, The Prisoner of Heaven is a majestic novel in which the threads of The Shadow of the Wind and The Angel's Game converge under the spell of literature and bring us toward the enigma of the mystery hidden at the heart of the Cemetery of Forgotten Books, a collection of lost treasures known only to its few initiates and the very core of Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n's enchanting fictional world.<br />(front flap)</span>

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/361100-do-i-need-to-read-the-angel-s-game-before">Do I need to read The Angel&#39;s Game before I can read this one? It seems like this is a continuation of Daniel&#39;s story from The Shadow of the Wind, while The Angel&#39;s Game is a completely separate story and character. While I be confused if I skip The Angel&#39;s Game and go straight onto The Prisoner of Heaven?</a>
</div>

<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/21334742-patrick-schl-ter">Patrick Schl√ºter</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I had read PoH before Angel's game and enjoyed it immensely. I re-read it after Angel's game and enjoyed it even more. The back-story of Angel's game <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">‚Ä¶more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I had read PoH before Angel's game and enjoyed it immensely. I re-read it after Angel's game and enjoyed it even more. The back-story of Angel's game filled the secondary characters with so much life that the story changed significantly. So I tend to think that the books should be read in the right order to be enjoyed to their fullest.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_418876073" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/418876073" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12913654-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407862957p2/12913654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/418876073?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/12913654-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12913654-ryan?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer418876073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16956367190166373043">This book is a perfect example of what is wrong with the publishing world. Zafon's first book, <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> is one of my all time favorites. Ever. The second book <i>The Angel's Game</i> is well up there on my list, so when I saw <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> on display, I was ecstatic. Unfortunately, it is very clear that Zafon was under time pressure from the publisher on this book, enough so that the story was irreversibly ruined. <br/><br/>You see, Zafon is a master of prose, character, and theme. Indeed </span>
  <span id="freeText16956367190166373043" style="display:none">This book is a perfect example of what is wrong with the publishing world. Zafon's first book, <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> is one of my all time favorites. Ever. The second book <i>The Angel's Game</i> is well up there on my list, so when I saw <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> on display, I was ecstatic. Unfortunately, it is very clear that Zafon was under time pressure from the publisher on this book, enough so that the story was irreversibly ruined. <br /><br />You see, Zafon is a master of prose, character, and theme. Indeed I would just say that Carlos Ruiz Zafon is a master storyteller in general, but he is also one that needs time to polish and work his product. The only problem is that because of the <b>HUGE</b> success of his other two books, the publisher (who's biggest concern is for money and not story) wanted a third book yesterday, not tomorrow, not next week, out with it! Some authors fight this or have a brilliant agent and publishing team behind them that believes in story rather than speed, not so with Zafon. Don't get me wrong, this book is still well written and at times brilliant, but in the end it is unfinished. This book is short, a mere 279 pages, compared to a solid 587 pages for <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> and 531 pages in <i>The Angels Game</i>. What this tells me as a reader is that Zafon wasn't even close to done and it shows. Where this book ends feels like the mid point of a longer book, it isn't really a conclusion, things aren't <i>really</i> tied together, but it was close enough to cut it off and call it "done". <br /><br />I did enjoy <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i>, but the rushed style, short story, and at times sloppy writing tells me that this once great author has been screwed by his publisher. Had this book had another year or even six months of work it would have been another shining example from one of the best writers of the 21st century. Instead we are left with a deep sense of betrayal at an incomplete story put out merely to make as much money as possible. Hopefully in the future Zafon is given the time necessary to produce books to his full potential and not merely for the quick cash. We will see I suppose.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16956367190166373043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3219077742" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3219077742" />
    <a title="Petrik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik"><img alt="Petrik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568016775p2/59458347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3219077742?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petrik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petrik" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik">Petrik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781780222851" href="/book/show/17655238-the-prisoner-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3219077742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3363185998126338107"><b>4.5/5 stars<br/><br/>Both Daniel Sempere and David Martin already have their respective background told, now it‚Äôs time for Fermin Romero de Torres‚Äôs past to be revealed.</b><br/><br/><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>‚ÄúOne mustn't dream of one's future; one must earn it.‚Äù </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br/><br/><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is the third‚Äîand penultimate‚Äîinstallment in <i>The Cemetery of Forgotten Books</i> series by Carlos Ruiz Zafon. The story takes place in Barcelona 1957, and it mainly revolves around connecting the plot-threads prepared in <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> and <i>The Ange</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3363185998126338107" style="display:none"><b>4.5/5 stars<br><br>Both Daniel Sempere and David Martin already have their respective background told, now it‚Äôs time for Fermin Romero de Torres‚Äôs past to be revealed.</b><br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>‚ÄúOne mustn't dream of one's future; one must earn it.‚Äù </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is the third‚Äîand penultimate‚Äîinstallment in <i>The Cemetery of Forgotten Books</i> series by Carlos Ruiz Zafon. The story takes place in Barcelona 1957, and it mainly revolves around connecting the plot-threads prepared in <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> and <i>The Angel‚Äôs Game</i>; at the same time, bringing new revelations and mysteries through Fermin‚Äôs past. It‚Äôs stated at the front page of the book that you can begin your journey with the series from whichever installment you choose; I highly disagree with this. I actually have no idea why that was stated, yes, technically you can understand the main story, but it is the nuances and familiarity with the characters established in the previous two books that made this book so damn engrossing. I strongly advise you to read this series in publication order. There were so many moments and scenes in this book that would lose their impact if you haven‚Äôt read the previous two books first.<br><br><b>Picture: <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> by Vincent Chong</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1590227183i/29526546._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I didn‚Äôt expect I would find myself this invested with the characters of the series, but the characters, at this stage of the series, have truly come alive for me. Daniel Sempere, David Martin, Julian Carax, Fermin, and many more side characters are fully fleshed-out characters with distinctive personalities and voices. In <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i>, we have known that Fermin‚Äîone of the character that made the first book astonishingly good‚Äîhad a damaged and harrowing past that was never conveyed to the readers; it is here we finally get to see the revelations of his dark past and how the story of the Sempere family, David Martin, and Fermin connects with each other. Daniel and Fermin‚Äôs friendship, which was one of the main highlights of the first book, returns in its full force and more due to the truth behind Fermin‚Äôs background, and I loved reading every page of this book.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>‚ÄúI've always thought that anyone who needs to join a herd so badly must be a bit of a sheep himself.‚Äù </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>I‚Äôve mentioned that <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> has a melancholic mood to the narrative, and <i>The Angel‚Äôs Game</i> retained a bleak and depressing tone throughout its story. <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> felt like the calm before the storm. The story and characterizations are spot-on, and the pacing was engrossing. Additionally, the low page count of the book made me finish reading it in one day. There‚Äôs no denying that this was very unputdownable, but I honestly would‚Äôve preferred this installment to be longer. One of the great things about the previous two books was how Zafon‚Äôs quality of narrative tightened with more pages read; there were contemplations, philosophical discussions, mysteries, relationships building, and the fiery passion for books. All of these, unfortunately, felt shortened or even missing due to the low page count.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>‚ÄúThe world's very small when you don't have anywhere to go.‚Äù </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>In a way, <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> felt like an impactful interlude that functions as a convergence of plotlines prior to proceeding towards the final installment of the series. Gripping, incredibly well-written (and translated) as always; my experience with this series so far already made me want to shout continuously that this is one of my favorite non-SFF series of all time, but I don‚Äôt want to jinx it now; I shall leave that bold claim until I‚Äôm done reading the entire series. The stages for the grand and twisted conclusion have been set, let‚Äôs find out whether <i>The Labyrinth of the Spirits</i> will be able to conclude this quartet satisfyingly or not.<br><br><b>You can order the book from: <u><a href="http://www.bookdepository.com/book/9780062206299/?a_aid=petrikleo" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Depository (Free shipping)</a></u></b><br><br><b>You can find <u><a href="http://novelnotions.net/2020/05/23/book-review-the-prisoner-of-heaven-the-cemetery-of-forgotten-books-3-by-carol-ruiz-zafon/" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a></u> and the rest of my reviews at <u><a href="http://novelnotions.net" rel="nofollow noopener">Novel Notions</a></u></b><br><br><i>Special thanks to my Patrons on <b><u><a href="https://www.patreon.com/petrikleo" rel="nofollow noopener">Patreon</a></u></b> for giving me extra support towards my passion for reading and reviewing!<br><br>My Patrons: Alfred, Devin, Hamad, Joie, Mike, Miracle, Nicholas.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3363185998126338107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_412591577" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/412591577" />
    <a title="Jeanette (Again)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-again"><img alt="Jeanette (Again)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304653755p2/796425.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/412591577?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanette (Again)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanette (Again)" href="/user/show/796425-jeanette-again">Jeanette (Again)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=four-star-fiction">four-star-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=europe-and-british-isles">europe-and-british-isles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/796425-jeanette-again?shelf=all-fiction">all-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412591577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1867458780752191582">Nothing can match the complexity and sinister suspense of The Shadow of the Wind, but The Prisoner of Heaven is a worthy follow-up to that story. I liked this one better than The Angel's Game, which I found somewhat confusing and overwrought. <br/><br/>Fermin Romero de Torres was my favorite character in The Shadow of the Wind(TSOTW), so I was delighted to discover that he takes center stage in this story. Remember in TSOTW when the boy Daniel meets Fermin, a pitiful, starving wraith in rags, prowling the</span>
  <span id="freeText1867458780752191582" style="display:none">Nothing can match the complexity and sinister suspense of The Shadow of the Wind, but The Prisoner of Heaven is a worthy follow-up to that story. I liked this one better than The Angel's Game, which I found somewhat confusing and overwrought. <br /><br />Fermin Romero de Torres was my favorite character in The Shadow of the Wind(TSOTW), so I was delighted to discover that he takes center stage in this story. Remember in TSOTW when the boy Daniel meets Fermin, a pitiful, starving wraith in rags, prowling the streets of Barcelona? The Prisoner of Heaven takes us back to the years after the Spanish Civil War, where we learn how Fermin came to be that wretch Daniel found. <br /><br />Daniel Sempere is all grown up now, married, and the father of a little boy. One day a creepy stranger enters Sempere and Son bookshop and leaves a cryptic note for Fermin. Daniel is both curious and worried for Fermin's safety, so Fermin begins to tell him the story of his years as a prisoner in Montjuic Castle. <br /><br />If you loved Fermin and his salacious one-liners, you're in for a treat. You'll also love the way Zafon ties in characters and events from both TSOTW and The Angel's Game. We revisit Beatriz, Bernarda, David Martin, and the Cemetery of Forgotten Books. We even get to learn a little more about Daniel Sempere's mother, Isabella, who died when Daniel was small. <br /><br />I loved the sweet and subtle way the book ends. Loved it. It's a perfect completion of the circle begun with the opening of The Shadow of the Wind. Zafon brings us back to where our journey began, while leaving the door open for further adventures.<br /><br />As ever, I'm grateful to translator Lucia Graves for making these stories available to us in English. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1867458780752191582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_371951099" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/371951099" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/371951099?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer371951099" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15642760159982824275"><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is the third in Carlos Ruiz Zafon‚Äôs series that began with <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> and continued with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60067703' rel='nofollow noopener'><i>The Angel‚Äôs Game</i>. </a> <br/><br/>The main character in this chapter of Ruiz Zafon‚Äôs multi-volume tale is Fermin Romero de Torres, friend to the Sempere family, the owners of a struggling bookshop, in 1957 Barcelona. While other characters get their time on stage, this is Fermin‚Äôs tale. And a compelling story it is, from the time he adopted his name, through his less than friendly encount</span>
  <span id="freeText15642760159982824275" style="display:none"><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is the third in Carlos Ruiz Zafon‚Äôs series that began with <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> and continued with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60067703" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>The Angel‚Äôs Game</i>. </a> <br /><br />The main character in this chapter of Ruiz Zafon‚Äôs multi-volume tale is Fermin Romero de Torres, friend to the Sempere family, the owners of a struggling bookshop, in 1957 Barcelona. While other characters get their time on stage, this is Fermin‚Äôs tale. And a compelling story it is, from the time he adopted his name, through his less than friendly encounters with Spanish fascists in 1939, including a stint in a top-of-the-hill prison that would have been at home in the mind of Kafka or Bram Stoker, to the present (1957) when a ghost from his time in prison comes calling. <br /><br />I confess that I found myself at loose ends a fair bit. As this is a continuation of the previous stories, or at the very least, is linked to them, I found myself, ironically, constantly straining to remember who this or that was, and what happened to them, or what it was that they had done. And even though I had read both prior books and kept notes on them, one of a thousand hard drive crashes had annihilated much of the information, and also, my note-taking was not quite so OCD as it is these days, so even the retained notes were of less than outstanding value. Maybe the best approach to Ruiz Zafon is to make a pile of all the books in the series and read them in a row, the better to keep things straight. <br /><br />Ruiz Zafon is trying to reconstruct the big novels of the 19th century. On his website, he specifically mentions Tolstoy, Dickens, and Wilkie Collins in discussing his aims. So there will be plenty of characters, plenty of sub-plots, and when spread over three (and eventually four) books it can be a bit much to keep track of, let alone recall from having read the prior volumes years before. <br /><br />The primary literary inspiration here is <i>The Count of Monte Cristo</i>, with both that novel and this one involving an undeserved imprisonment, a clever escape, fabulous treasure, and adventure. The mention is overt in the text and gives us a neon clue as to how a prison escape will be accomplished. <br /><br />While this is a page-turner to read, it is more than a mere action-adventure treat. There is content lurking in the shadows. Keep in mind that one of the central images of the entire series is a literal place called the ‚ÄúCemetery of Forgotten Books.‚Äù It does not figure very largely in this episode, but it underlies. The need to remember, however, permeates. And it is not just the desire to keep alive the memory of loved ones, or even of literature, but of the reality of Fascism, lest the powers that be erase that memory.<br /><br />Christmas figures early in the story, an image of hopefulness in a time almost worth forgetting:<blockquote>In those days, Christmas still retained a certain aura of magic and mystery. The powdery light of winter, the hopeful expressions of people who lived among the shadows and silences, lent that setting a slight air of promise in which, at least children and those who had learned the art of forgetting, could still believe.</blockquote>Memory is erased as the corrupt surround themselves with their kind then intimidate speakers of truth into suppressing reality and promoting fiction. Although this is not at all referenced here I could not help but think of the McCarthy era in America, and today of those who claim a perfect knowledge of the American founders as a defense of their positions, and any and all political spinmeisters.<blockquote>I don‚Äôt know where I‚Äôve read that deep down we‚Äôve never been who we think we once were, and we only remember what never happened</blockquote>Lies find their way into common experience<blockquote>I‚Äôve seen a few cases and the patients often hear voices, or they see and remember people and events that have never taken place‚Ä¶The mind slowly deteriorates and the patient can no longer distinguish between reality and fiction.<br /><br />‚Äúlike seventy percent of Spaniards. ‚Äú</blockquote>And a bow to the importance of historians, research and writing:<blockquote>cities have no memory and they need someone like me, a sage with his feet on the ground, to keep it alive</blockquote>Cemeteries of varying sorts pop up like mushrooms after a shower, Fermin has a close encounter with a particularly grisly one, there is the cemetery of forgotten books of course, and other visits take place as well. They seem to be locales where, ironically, truth is kept alive.<br /><br />Fire flames into the text many times, but I leave that for another reviewer<br /><br />And I take the title to refer nor only the Daniel Martin‚Äôs dubious mental state, but to the person of Spain itself, which, in a way, had to keep its own consciousness in the clouds in order to survive the horrors of fascism.<br /><br />While I may have concerns about one‚Äôs ability to read this book solo, this is not to say that this was not an entertaining read. It was. It is not to say that the characters are not interesting. They are, at least the ones which are developed. And it is not to say that this book cannot be read as a stand-alone. It can, although it would be like reading with one eye tied behind your back. (Yes, I know) I do wish, though, that more ink had been applied to the female characters. They seem to serve here more as plot devices than as realized individuals. <br /><br />So, bottom line here is that <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is an engaging and entertaining read, offering the perceptive reader plenty of content beneath the surface story, connections to literature from the past and an appreciation of the importance of keeping the truth alive in our memory. I would suggest, however, having the first two volumes in the series close at hand, or ideally, if you have not read them already, read all three at once for the best possible immersive reading experience. Forgetting is a terrible thing.<br /><br />PS - You might enjoy checking out the <a href="http://www.carlosruizzafon.co.uk/" rel="nofollow noopener">author's site</a><br /><br />I wish there were half stars. I wanted to give this 3.5<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15642760159982824275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8964717606977087423" style="display:none">The book to me was really like Ruiz Zafon decided that he had all these notes and back story on Fermin Romero de Torres and how could you turn that into a novel. Well you link some of the previous characters into the story and produce a book that leaves you as the reader disappointed. This book, while explaining away some back story did nothing for the trilogy except open up the possibility of another book. I did not find myself engaged with the characters nor all that intrigued by the mysterious stranger. <br />As I write this I am still conflicted about what the point of this book was. I mean it tied up some loose ends but did it need to? Does everything have to be so black and white in story-telling? <br />The other thing is the blurb of this book fails big time. The stranger will lead Daniel deep into the enigma at the heart of the Cemetery of Forgotten Books." If only that story was contained in the book and if only there was that level of mystery in the book. <br />I feel that blurb promised more than what was actually in the book and that is where the disappointment for me lies. <br />I have to admit I loved the first two books but this novel. I can not explain why I feel so let down, the writing was great everything was great but the story was more like a filler.</span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780297868095" href="/book/show/13369595-the-prisoner-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50581-lydia-presley?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50581-lydia-presley?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50581-lydia-presley?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345348796" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16201871759852193013">Books like The Prisoner of Heaven by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n make me love being not only a reader, but someone who loves the look, feel, and smell of books. Why? Because I get the feeling that Zaf√≥n has the same sort of reaction to picking up a book. There's this feeling of history, companionship, and shared experience I get when I handle something old and precious, and a sense of awakening hope for the future when I pick up something new. <br/><br/>The Prisoner of Heaven is the third book centering around the </span>
  <span id="freeText16201871759852193013" style="display:none">Books like The Prisoner of Heaven by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n make me love being not only a reader, but someone who loves the look, feel, and smell of books. Why? Because I get the feeling that Zaf√≥n has the same sort of reaction to picking up a book. There's this feeling of history, companionship, and shared experience I get when I handle something old and precious, and a sense of awakening hope for the future when I pick up something new. <br /><br />The Prisoner of Heaven is the third book centering around the Cemetery of Forgotten Books, but if you haven't read the other two novels, The Shadow of the Wind and The Angel's Game, don't let that stop you from picking this one up - because you see, Zaf√≥n has done something brilliant and perfectly fitting with these books. You can start with any book and read them in any order, and they all remain connected through this one, single, perfect place. In this book the story of Fermin Romero de Torres is detailed out piece by fascinating piece, and Daniel is given more information on the history of his parents. The relationship between Daniel and Bea is also in question - and references to both The Shadow of the Wind and The Angel's Game crop up throughout the book in, sometimes, the most surprising of places.<br /><br />And let's not forget the fantastic homage paid to The Count of Monte Cristo - because it's there and it's a beautiful thing. Zaf√≥n has this way of describing places that are detestable, filled with dirt and filth and corruption, and making it come to life in such a way that I was both fascinated and repulsed at the same time. But then to tie in the literary message - it was a thing of beauty and I cannot stop raving over how good this book was. <br /><br />I devoured The Prisoner of Heaven in about four hours. It is less than 300 pages, and more easily accessible, reading wise, than Zaf√≥n's previous books were. His prose is still beautiful - full of flowing lyricism that made me feel, at times, as if I were reading a piece of artwork, but it was more simple, less complicated somehow. Then again, it may just have been the fantastic story being told.<br /><br />If you haven't checked out Zaf√≥n's books, or are avoiding them because of the hype, don't. Please. I'm not one to brim with praises for hyped books, but I can say that these deserve every bit. </span>

<div id="review_1400805908" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1400805908" />
    <a title="Kaya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15462098-kaya"><img alt="Kaya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485382914p2/15462098.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1400805908?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaya" href="/user/show/15462098-kaya">Kaya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1400805908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5294458945604828084"><b>
  <i>‚ÄúDeep down we've never been who we think we once were, and we only remember what never happened.‚Äù </i>
</b><br/><br/>I didn‚Äôt expect this book to have such a dark turn. And that says a lot after melancholic Shadow of the Wind and depressive Angel‚Äôs Game. The author took the best out of the previous two books and made a true masterpiece. While my favorite Daniel isn‚Äôt the star of the book, Fermin was more than a competent protagonist. <br/><br/>Daniel Sem¬≠pere and his wife Bea are con¬≠tent with their marriage and their b</span>
  <span id="freeText5294458945604828084" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>‚ÄúDeep down we've never been who we think we once were, and we only remember what never happened.‚Äù </i>
</b><br /><br />I didn‚Äôt expect this book to have such a dark turn. And that says a lot after melancholic Shadow of the Wind and depressive Angel‚Äôs Game. The author took the best out of the previous two books and made a true masterpiece. While my favorite Daniel isn‚Äôt the star of the book, Fermin was more than a competent protagonist. <br /><br />Daniel Sem¬≠pere and his wife Bea are con¬≠tent with their marriage and their baby Julian. Daniel‚Äôs best friend, Fermin is about to get mar¬≠ried, but when a creepy stranger walks into Sempere's bookshop and leaves a cryptic note for Fermin, everything is about to fall apart. Daniel is both curious and worried about Fermin's safety, so Fermin has no other choice than to tell him the story of his years as a prisoner in Montjuic Castle. While Shadow of the Wind is still the best book in the series, this one is the most crucial as it enables the reader to understand everything that has happened so far. It links the two previous two books together. Many unanswered questions from both The Shadow Of The Wind and The Angels Game are answered and it still managed to end with a cliffhanger.<br /><br />I loved the sense of familiarity I have with Daniel, David, and Fermin, though this ride was quite painful. Although the plot isn't as layered and mysterious as The Shadow of the Wind, the novel moves along at a rapid pace and still manages to make your heart beat faster. Zaf√≥n has such a way with words that when he describes places filled with dirt, filth, and corruption, he makes it so vivid and realistic that I feel both amazed and repulsed. Honestly, these characters became part of me. <br /><br />Half of the enjoyment of this book is Fermin's clever lines and observations about people and existentialism. He‚Äôs a complex, extravagant and secretive person, but most of all he‚Äôs a loyal friend and full of love. With everything he‚Äôs been through, it‚Äôs a miracle he‚Äôs such a gentle and warm person. <br /><br /><i>‚ÄúA man who could urinate standing up and without help was a man in a fit state to face his responsibilities.‚Äù</i><br /><br />Fermin almost succumbed to despair and conditions in prison, and there had been humorous moments. Out of all Zafon‚Äôs book I‚Äôve read so far, this one had the funniest and light moments. It‚Äôs amazing how it‚Äôs both his darkest and lightest book. Contradictions and grey areas are his comfort zone<br /> <br /><i>‚ÄúI've always thought that anyone who needs to join a herd so badly must be a bit of a sheep himself.‚Äù</i><br /><br />The whole story is magnificent. <br /><br />Daniel is, once again, on a mission to defend his loved ones, while his darker side is uncovering within his family-man personality. His growing anxieties, jealousy, trauma from the past experiences and his distrust of those closest to him may show more dangerous to his perfect life than he wants to let it. <br /><br /><i>‚ÄúOne mustn't dream of one's future; one must earn it.‚Äù</i><br /><br />And that‚Äôs the journey Daniel has to face throughout the whole book. <br /><br /><i>Daniel‚Äôs</i> detective work leads him to dig out a story from Fermin of survival. Their friendship is one of the strongest elements in the whole series and probably one of the best bromances I‚Äôve ever read about. They support each other and even give tough love when necessary. Their friendship especially pays off by the end of the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5294458945604828084" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2687901454" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2687901454" />
    <a title="TS Chan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30279227-ts-chan"><img alt="TS Chan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543800303p2/30279227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2687901454?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TS Chan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TS Chan" href="/user/show/30279227-ts-chan">TS Chan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18900805-the-prisoner-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2687901454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17380239242882874888"><b>4.5 stars<br/><br/><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> was another captivating entry in <i>The Cemetery of Forgotten Books</i> that provided a more balanced tone of light and dark.</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>‚ÄúDestiny doesn't do home visits... you have to go for it yourself.‚Äù</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br/>The third book of the series felt almost like an interlude between the wider narrative, especially because of its much shorter length. Fans of the marvellous Ferm√¨n Romero de Torres will rejoice to know that <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> provided his much-needed backstory. It w</span>
  <span id="freeText17380239242882874888" style="display:none"><b>4.5 stars<br /><br /><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> was another captivating entry in <i>The Cemetery of Forgotten Books</i> that provided a more balanced tone of light and dark.</b><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>‚ÄúDestiny doesn't do home visits... you have to go for it yourself.‚Äù</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br />The third book of the series felt almost like an interlude between the wider narrative, especially because of its much shorter length. Fans of the marvellous Ferm√¨n Romero de Torres will rejoice to know that <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> provided his much-needed backstory. It would find it hard to believe that any reader who loved <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> would not consider Ferm√¨n as a favourite character. In the shadows and ashes of the city of the damned, Ferm√¨n glows and shines like a beacon of hope and goodness. A wholly good and kind-hearted person who has a weakness for ladies and strong opinions on pretty much everything, his eloquent and really long ramblings are always sardonic, hilarious and pretty spot on.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>‚ÄúDeep down we've never been who we think we once were, and we only remember what never happened.‚Äù</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br />However, the title of the book does not refer to Ferm√¨n at all, but to someone else whom he met in the prison of Montjuic Castle during the Spanish Civil War. Arising from this backstory of Ferm√¨n, narrative threads that connect the stories and characters within the series was revealed. The scenes which took place in the prison was really horrifying, and out of this account there rose a villainous figure of such despicable character as to challenge the monstrous Inspector Fumero from the first book.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>‚ÄúThose times, against all expectations, were turning out to be good times. Then he felt afraid, because he knew they couldn't last long and those stolen drops of happiness and peace would evaporate.‚Äù</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br />As I've mentioned above, this book has a more balanced tone. Despite the harrowing accounts of Ferm√¨n's life in prison, the current timeline of the story was blessed with happier, lighthearted moments that helped to break the heavy-hearted narrative. While this book felt a bit too short to deliver the same level of emotional tension and impact as the previous volumes, it nevertheless managed to still resonate with me because of the continuity of the character development. And of course, the writing and its translation remained as beautiful as ever.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>‚ÄúIn those days, Christmas still retained a certain aura of magic and mystery. The powdery light of winter, the hopeful expressions of people who lived among shadows and silence, lent that setting a slight air of promise in which at least children and those who had learned the art of forgetting could still believe.‚Äù</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br />The closing scene in the Epilogue of <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> builds up the anticipation for the final volume of The Cemetery of Forgotten Books to dizzying heights. I could feel it in my bones that it is going to be an incredible and memorable one, and as I cannot wait to finish this series, I also dread to arrive at its end. Such is the price of reading truly special books.<br /><br /><b>You can order the book from: <u><a href="https://www.bookdepository.com/Prisoner-Heaven-Carlos-Ruiz-Zafon/9781780222851/?a_aid=petrikleo" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Depository (Free shipping)</a></u> | <u><a href="https://bookshop.org/books/the-prisoner-of-heaven/9780062206299" rel="nofollow noopener">Bookshop.org</a></u></b><br /><br /><i>You can find <a href="https://wp.me/pa0WBY-1U3" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a> and my other reviews at <a href="http://novelnotions.net/category/book-reviews/tss-reviews/" rel="nofollow noopener">Novel Notions.</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17380239242882874888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText6688939139671910985" style="display:none">I loved this trilogy , (tetralogy, since I have now found out there is a final book that just came out , only in Spanish for not , lucky for me I read Spanish too :) ) but I digress, this third book didn't feel whole for me , it felt like there were parts missing , like somebody had a 600 page book, and then edited 350 pages out of it thinking that it made sense , while all the core elements that have made me love this series are there (the cemetery of forgotten books, the gothic mystery, recurring characters and Madrid as background) the story feels rushed and without a goal in mind , it feels more to me like a transitionary book whose main goal is to gather buzz for the next book. I look forward to the next book (hopefully the end of these series) while this third book didn't satisfy me , I still highly recommend this tetralogy to anyone (the shadow of the wind, the angels game, the prisoner of heaven) and I still haven't found the name of the fourth. It is like no book out there.</span>

<div id="review_365364502" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/365364502" />
    <a title="Zach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6022933-zach"><img alt="Zach" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/365364502?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zach" href="/user/show/6022933-zach">Zach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6022933-zach?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6022933-zach?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6022933-zach?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer365364502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer452470521446527287">I finish this book a bit confused. First of all, the discrepancies between this and The Angel's Game, specifically concerning the events and time frame of Isabella's death, left me scratching my head. Did CRZ do that specifically in order to confound the reader and then rescue his curiosity in the last book? I hope it is something like that, because there are only 2 other alternatives that are equally distasteful to me. The first is that I actually made the mistake in ordering all the events tha</span>
  <span id="freeText452470521446527287" style="display:none">I finish this book a bit confused. First of all, the discrepancies between this and The Angel's Game, specifically concerning the events and time frame of Isabella's death, left me scratching my head. Did CRZ do that specifically in order to confound the reader and then rescue his curiosity in the last book? I hope it is something like that, because there are only 2 other alternatives that are equally distasteful to me. The first is that I actually made the mistake in ordering all the events that happened in TAG and didn't reconcile them properly with The Prisoner of Heaven. Secondly, it could be CRZ just got lazy and just decided to ignore his epilogue in TAG. Either way, this is definitely a build-up to the fourth installment, and what a build-up it was. TPoH lacked the compelling storyline that was such a huge part of TSotW. All this did was plant the seeds that could blossom into a fucking fantastic final book. Or, his epilogue from TPoH could trap him again for this fourth book and he will either have to divulge in anachronistic and conflicting plot lines again (if my understanding is correct) or make up lies. So basically, and although this sounds weird and unorthodox, my rating of this book is not complete and hinges entirely on the last book, and god know's when that will come out. One thing that I did love about this book was the fact that people who have read The Count of Monte Cristo and Les Miserables get an extra layer of understanding for this book, and this makes the 3 months I put into the latter a little more rewarding. But, based on my high expectations, I can only give this book 3 stars. CRZ just better come through in that last book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="452470521446527287" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2189220522" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14339639480704997111">I think this one is even better that the first one, or at least they both are equal. Fermin always was my favorite character in this story and this book it's basically about him. The writing, the plot, that sense of humor! I cant wait for the last book, so much tension!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14191978352519864764">Original review(s) below - seems I did one on Spanish publication in 2011 and then a FBC review linked on English publication, so now just a few thoughts on the reread in January 2017 leading to the 4th and last book in the series <br/><br/>- book still entertaining and a fast read that started strong and ended on a sort of cliffhanger <br/>- shorter and more of a set-up; now that the David Martin revelation is known (which was the shock of the novel on first read) the book reads somewhat differently as the fo</span>
  <span id="freeText14191978352519864764" style="display:none">Original review(s) below - seems I did one on Spanish publication in 2011 and then a FBC review linked on English publication, so now just a few thoughts on the reread in January 2017 leading to the 4th and last book in the series <br /><br />- book still entertaining and a fast read that started strong and ended on a sort of cliffhanger <br />- shorter and more of a set-up; now that the David Martin revelation is known (which was the shock of the novel on first read) the book reads somewhat differently as the focus moves to Daniel's ongoing story, while the Fermin/David Martin past seems less immediate (maybe this is influenced by the way the 4th book Laberinto de los Espiritus goes to start with)<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Original review(s) 2011-2:<br />(thoughts in Nov 20111 from the Spanish lang edition) Some weeks ago the third installment of the awesome series that started with The Shadow of the Wind and continued with The Angel's Game has been published in Spain. As FBC is an English language site, I usually refrain from talking about books that are not available in English, but in this case as the UK publication of "El Prisionero del Cielo" has been tentatively set on June 2nd, 2012 and presumably the US publication will soon follow, I will note several things about the novel with a full review to follow next year as I sometimes do with (English language) advance review copies of great interest.<br /><br />"El Prisionero del Cielo" is shorter than the previous books and is also thematically different, forgoing the dual - past/present - love affairs of the first two books. Also while the book keeps the past/present threads, this time they are clearly divided with Daniel and Fermin as the respective narrators. As the title character is David Martin (!), it should be clear that familiarity with both books is necessary and I found myself darting back and forth through Shadow of the Wind and The Angel's Game too.<br /><br />Great prose, great characters and all that you expect from a CR Zafon novel, the one niggle I had is that "El Prisionero del Cielo" is the least self-contained of the novels so far both as backstory goes which I noted above and with an ending that while not quite a cliffhanger, definitely begs the fourth and presumably last "Cemetery of Forgotten Books" novel.<br /><br />Still a clear Top 25 novel of 2011 for me and I am really looking forward to the English language version to see the subtleties I missed.<br /><br />Currently reading the Engl. lang. version<br /><br />As impressive as I remembered from the Spanish version; full rv in a few days<br /><br />Here is the FBC Rv link:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://fantasybookcritic.blogspot.com/2012/07/prisoner-of-heaven-by-carlos-ruiz-zafon.html">http://fantasybookcritic.blogspot.com...</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11618721980493516188"><em>The year is 1957. It‚Äôs just before Christmas in Barcelona and Daniel and Bea are living with their son above the bookstore Sempere & Sons. The faithful servant Ferm√≠n is about to get married. One day an old man visits the bookstore and he wants to buy an old and valuable version of The Count of Monte Cristo. But he doesn‚Äôt take the book with him instead he leaves the books with a dedication‚Ä¶</em><br/><br/>The book continues the story that started in <strong>The Shadow of the Wind</strong> and also has ties with <strong>The Angel's Gam</strong></span>
  <span id="freeText11618721980493516188" style="display:none"><em>The year is 1957. It‚Äôs just before Christmas in Barcelona and Daniel and Bea are living with their son above the bookstore Sempere &amp; Sons. The faithful servant Ferm√≠n is about to get married. One day an old man visits the bookstore and he wants to buy an old and valuable version of The Count of Monte Cristo. But he doesn‚Äôt take the book with him instead he leaves the books with a dedication‚Ä¶</em><br /><br />The book continues the story that started in <strong>The Shadow of the Wind</strong> and also has ties with <strong>The Angel's Game</strong> which story takes place before <strong>The Shadow of the Wind</strong>. In many ways it‚Äôs a really good story, you get to know what happened to David Martin from <strong>The Angel's Game</strong> and his connection to the Sempere family. Well, you don‚Äôt get all the answers, the ending has a ‚Äúto be continued‚Äù feeling over itself. And since the book had only 300 pages (the Swedish version) and I just think Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n could have written well at least 100-200 pages more and just given the book a better ending instead of leaving one hanging. Well, it‚Äôs a smart move because now one just has to have the next book‚Ä¶ when it comes‚Ä¶<br /><br /><strong>The Angel's Game</strong> was such a great book that really pulled you inside and this book for all its promises didn‚Äôt really reach the same level, it was good and I love the connection to <strong>The Count of Monte Cristo</strong> (need to re-read the book), loved to know what happened to David Martin, how and where he wrote his book. I just want more‚Ä¶more Daniel Sempere and more David Martin‚Ä¶more about the Cemetery of Forgotten Books...</span>

<div id="review_372922275" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/372922275" />
    <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers"><img alt="TheBookSmugglers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316717753p2/1223249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/372922275?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TheBookSmugglers" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers">TheBookSmugglers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer372922275" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6798967823280578498">Original review posted on <a href='http://thebooksmugglers.com/2012/07/book-review-the-prisoner-of-heaven-by-carlos-ruiz-zafon.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book Smugglers</a><br/><br/>WARNING! This post contains spoilers!!<br/><br/>Oh, The Prisoner of Heaven, WHY? WHY?<br/><br/>You will have to excuse this sudden display of emotional distress. But I am overcome with feelings and they must be exorcized here.<br/><br/>A few years ago I read The Shadow of the Wind, a tremendous Gothic thriller with labyrinthine storytelling, a wonderful sense of setting, beautiful writing and incredible characters. It soon became a favourite. I do recognise its problems especially </span>
  <span id="freeText6798967823280578498" style="display:none">Original review posted on <a href="http://thebooksmugglers.com/2012/07/book-review-the-prisoner-of-heaven-by-carlos-ruiz-zafon.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book Smugglers</a><br /><br />WARNING! This post contains spoilers!!<br /><br />Oh, The Prisoner of Heaven, WHY? WHY?<br /><br />You will have to excuse this sudden display of emotional distress. But I am overcome with feelings and they must be exorcized here.<br /><br />A few years ago I read The Shadow of the Wind, a tremendous Gothic thriller with labyrinthine storytelling, a wonderful sense of setting, beautiful writing and incredible characters. It soon became a favourite. I do recognise its problems especially those concerning the depiction of female characters but since everything else about that book was so good, I was able to enjoy it to a great extent. That said, I always felt that the The Shadow of the Wind was a standalone novel, its main story a self-contained affair ‚Äì even though there was indeed potential for more stories in that world.<br /><br />Cue a few years later and the author published a prequel entitled The Angel‚Äôs Game. Although the same beautiful prose was present, the book turned out to be a great disappointment. A meandering plot, clich√©d characters and a GOTCHA open-for-interpretation clumsy ending and a very loose connection to The Shadow of the Wind made the cynical in me believe The Angel‚Äôs Game to be an afterthought to capitalise on the huge success of that first book.<br /><br />Cue to a few years later and we now have The Prisoner of Heaven. A small introduction tells us that this is part of a cycle of novels in the same literary universe and this labyrinth of stories ‚Äúwhen woven together lead to the heart of the narrative‚Äù.<br /><br />The Prisoner of Heaven is then, as a matter of fact, the third book in what is now, excuse my French, a freaking quartet!<br /><br />In all fairness there is nothing wrong with this idea in principle but in reality, I find its execution to be clumsy and exploitative.<br /><br />The Prisoner of Heaven is narrated once more by The Shadow of the Wind‚Äôs hero Daniel Sempere ‚Äì now married to Bea and with a young child, running the Sempere and Sons bookstore alongside his father. Business is not good and the two Semperes are struggling to make ends meet. Happiness is on the horizon though, as their close friend Ferm√≠n Romero de Torres is about to wed Bernarda, the love of his life. But there is something bothering Ferm√≠n and he is not his usual upbeat self. Just then, a strange figure walks into the bookstore and leaves a message for Ferm√≠n that says:<br /><br />‚ÄúFor Ferm√≠n Romero de Torres, who came back from among the dead and holds the key to the future.‚Äù<br /><br />This prompts Daniel to question Ferm√≠n about his past and those revelations will change everything Daniel knows about not only Ferm√≠n but also about his own family.<br /><br />Ostensibly, The Prisoner of Heaven is Ferm√≠n‚Äôs book. The vast majority of the book is his account of his prison time at the infamous Montju√Øc castle where during the days of Franco‚Äôs dictatorship (in the 40s), political prisoners where held and ‚Äúdisappeared‚Äù. The depiction of the prison and how the prisoners suffered was horrific and affecting.<br /><br />It is there that Ferm√≠n becomes friends with David Martin, no other than the narrator of The Angel‚Äôs Game (!!!!!) (I shall go back to this astounding piece of information later). I have to say though that the description of the horrors suffered by the prisoners inside the prison were completely cheapened by Ferm√≠n‚Äôs daredevil escape ‚Äì in the fashion of Count of Monte Cristo, a scheme devised and organised by David Martin. On that front one must ask: why didn‚Äôt David organise his own escape? Because it didn‚Äôt suit this story, that‚Äôs why.<br /><br />Mind you, there are good things about The Prisoner of Heaven. The writing is still captivating and Ferm√≠n is his usual lovable self. In fact, Ferm√≠n remains a favourite character of mine. His resilience, his heroism, his wits and sense of humour are what make The Prisoner of Heaven at least readable.<br /><br />The Prisoner of Heaven has a series of mysteries that are investigated by Daniel and Ferm√≠n and this construct as well as the very nature of those mysteries were utterly familiar. The ‚Äúwho is this figure who left the message‚Äù, the ‚Äúwhy is this happening‚Äù, the ‚Äúvillain of this story is a piece of shit policeman‚Äù are just SO MUCH like The Shadow of the Wind it‚Äôs not even funny.<br /><br />But the two main problems I had with The Prisoner of Heaven are the way that it rewrites the story of the first two books, and the depiction of the female characters.<br /><br />With regards to the former: all of a sudden, we learn that through his friendship with David Martin, Ferm√≠n has been a part of the Semperes‚Äô life ALL ALONG. That he knew about them, and has been following Daniel since he was a child. All of a sudden, Daniel‚Äôs mother Isabella was murdered as a part of this plot. All of a sudden, David Martin himself is a hero and we are led to question once again his already extremely unreliable narrative in The Angel‚Äôs Game. I get the feeling that this is supposed to enrich the story, to deepen it with the promised ‚Äúnarrative links‚Äù of the introduction. In reality, to me, this has done nothing but to cheapen the stories told so far. I do appreciate that this is a very personal reaction though and I am sure other readers will feel differently.<br /><br />Which brings me to the problematic depiction of the female characters. Although I was able to love the first book despite this obvious problem, I found that this trend has been amplified in The Prisoner of Heaven in a way that it jumps from the pages. The vast majority of the female characters in this series are either prostitutes with a Heart of Gold (always, always described as being past ‚Äútheir prime‚Äù, poor souls to be pitied and lied to about their crumbling beauty) or Impossibly Beautiful Tragic/Angelic figures. Most of the female characters are victims. They are there to be protected, avenged or suspected of foul play.<br /><br />One of the secondary storylines depicts Daniel finding a letter in Bea‚Äôs possession ‚Äì which, by the way, he opens and reads‚Äì and when it turns out to be a love letter from her ex-fianc√©e, Bea becomes the object of Daniel‚Äôs suspicion. This is never fully addressed in the narrative directly with Bea, who never finds out about his suspicions. It is always about Daniel, about what he will do, how he will deal with this. He suspects her because she is too beautiful. To add insult to the injury, toward the end of the novel, there is one random chapter when the narrative is from Bernarda and Bea‚Äôs PoV and it is entirely about the men in their lives.<br /><br />Ultimately, The Prisoner of Heaven reads like a filler novella. It is short, its narrative is full of shortcuts, it is nowhere near as well executed as The Shadow of the Wind and most exasperating of all, it ends on a maddening cliff-hanger in preparation for the final book.<br /><br />I feel no desire to read the last book in this (now) quartet. In fact, I am done with Zafon‚Äôs books for good. It should tell you something that instead of feeling sad, I actually feel relieved for having made this decision. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6798967823280578498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2789683826" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer858145807892112448">In September 2018 Zafon released the fourth, and final, episode of his series featuring the <i>Cemetery of Forgotten Books</i>, a labyrinthine library of obscure and forgotten titles hidden away in the old city of Barcelona. Those lucky enough to be invited into its enclave are permitted to choose just one book from its shelves. In book 1, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232.The_Shadow_of_the_Wind__The_Cemetery_of_Forgotten_Books___1_' title='The Shadow of the Wind (The Cemetery of Forgotten Books, #1) by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Shadow of the Wind</a> we meet ten-year-old Daniel who has been granted this rare privilege and in due course readers drawn into a world of mystery and intrigue str</span>
  <span id="freeText858145807892112448" style="display:none">In September 2018 Zafon released the fourth, and final, episode of his series featuring the <i>Cemetery of Forgotten Books</i>, a labyrinthine library of obscure and forgotten titles hidden away in the old city of Barcelona. Those lucky enough to be invited into its enclave are permitted to choose just one book from its shelves. In book 1, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232.The_Shadow_of_the_Wind__The_Cemetery_of_Forgotten_Books___1_" title="The Shadow of the Wind (The Cemetery of Forgotten Books, #1) by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Shadow of the Wind</a> we meet ten-year-old Daniel who has been granted this rare privilege and in due course readers drawn into a world of mystery and intrigue stretching from 1938 through to the 1970s. The author is on record as having stated that the books can be read in any order, allowing the reader to get to the heart of the narrative via different paths. This may be true but having read, some years ago, the first two books in the series I thought it wise to grab a copy of the rather short book three before launching myself into the massive final tome.<br /><br />In this episode the story is picked up in 1957 and here the central character is really Daniel‚Äôs friend Fermin. Daniel is working in his father‚Äôs book shop one day when a mysterious figure appears and proceeds to purchase a unique, and expensive, illustrated edition of <i>The Count of Monte Cristo</i> before adding a cryptic inscription addressed to Fermin. Leaving delivery of the book to Daniel he exits the shop. As events begin to unfold from this point we learn much more of Fermin‚Äôs history, and in itself this is an interesting tale. Many of the characters here will be familiar to readers who have previously visited this series and whilst the story here is to some extent self contained it really represents another piece in the larger jigsaw puzzle. <br /><br />I did enjoy this book, it‚Äôs strange and atmospheric and the characters are expertly drawn. I finished it too quickly, but at least I have the final book to look forward to. I really can‚Äôt wait to dive back into the world inhabited Daniel, his family, his friends and those that live in the shadows too. As for the idea of reading these books in any order, for me reading them in the order they were published makes sense, but I can see that there is sufficient overlap and self containment to suggest that addressing them in a different sequence might work equally well. If you‚Äôve missed out on these books so far but enjoy mysteries set in times past, then do give this series a try ‚Äì I have a sense you wont regret it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="858145807892112448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText14788625048545242271" style="display:none">Because it was chosen for my women's only book club, I read the first novel in this series called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232.The_Shadow_of_the_Wind__The_Cemetery_of_Forgotten_Books___1_" title="The Shadow of the Wind (The Cemetery of Forgotten Books, #1) by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shadow of the Wind</a>. <div id="review_237459987" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/237459987" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/237459987?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/15719269-the-prisoner-of-heaven">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Existing fans of the series only</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=2012-release">2012-release</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=2011-release">2011-release</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=read-on-kindle">read-on-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=macabre-slipstream-weird">macabre-slipstream-weird</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer237459987" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5661945943326474762"><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is the third book in Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n's Barcelona quartet, which began with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232.The_Shadow_of_the_Wind' title='The Shadow of the Wind by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Shadow of the Wind</a> and continued with its prequel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4912857.The_Angel_s_Game' title='The Angel's Game by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Angel's Game</a>. This story draws strands of the two previous books together, revisiting Daniel Sempere, the protagonist of <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i>, and his friend and colleague Ferm√≠n Romero de Torres. Ferm√≠n, previously more of an amusing sidekick, takes centre stage here as his complex and dramatic backstory is revealed (although Daniel sti</span>
  <span id="freeText5661945943326474762" style="display:none"><i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is the third book in Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n's Barcelona quartet, which began with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232.The_Shadow_of_the_Wind" title="The Shadow of the Wind by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shadow of the Wind</a> and continued with its prequel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4912857.The_Angel_s_Game" title="The Angel's Game by Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n" rel="nofollow noopener">The Angel's Game</a>. This story draws strands of the two previous books together, revisiting Daniel Sempere, the protagonist of <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i>, and his friend and colleague Ferm√≠n Romero de Torres. Ferm√≠n, previously more of an amusing sidekick, takes centre stage here as his complex and dramatic backstory is revealed (although Daniel still acts as the primary narrator). As Daniel listens to this tale, the reader is slowly made aware of the connections between the characters of all three books. <br /><br />Although it's been a while since I read the previous books, I did at least remember fragments of the stories and a general idea of what had happened, and I can't imagine how this book would read to those unfamiliar with the other two. Because of this, I wouldn't really recommend it unless you've read <i>The Shadow of the Wind</i> and/or <i>The Angel's Game</i> first. Those who have enjoyed either of the other books will find many of their best attributes recreated here - a mysterious, gothic version of Barcelona populated with eccentric and larger-than-life characters, an instantly compelling beginning in which a sinister figure does something disconcerting, sparking a chain of events which includes flashbacks to the past, the dredging up of dark secrets, love and death, etc. <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is quite short, and although this means some scope for more detailed description has been skipped (and there's much less discussion of literary and philosophical concepts than in the previous books), it does help the plot to move quickly, in brief, snappy chapters.<br /><br />However, for those who have read <i>The Angel's Game</i>... I'm a little bit confused. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The version of what happens to David Mart√≠n in this book appears to contradict the conclusion of his story in <i>The Angel's Game</i>. What about the 'happy ending' he was supposedly granted by Andreas Corelli? What on earth happened to Cristina? She was the love of David's life in the previous book, but isn't even mentioned in this one, and suddenly he's madly in love with Isabella, who was previously portrayed as his best friend/confidante?! I have no idea whether this was meant to be deliberate, whether it perhaps indicates that David's own testament was unreliable (totally plausible given the nature of that ending, but still confusing), or whether the truth will be revealed in the final book of the series. Either way, it left me wondering exactly what I was supposed to think, and as other reviewers have remarked, it means it's going to be seriously difficult for the author to resolve all of these different threads in the concluding part. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />There are still things I don't like about these books, namely the humour and the way the female characters are portrayed/described, both of which have always seemed out of place to me amongst the themes and style of the stories. The other problem with <i>The Prisoner of Heaven</i> is its brevity: it's more like half a book, or a novella designed to act as filler before the next 'proper' installment is published. However, I can't deny that it was hugely readable and I didn't want to put it down - in fact, I barely did from the moment I started reading, and it must have only taken me a couple of sittings, within less than 24 hours, to get it finished.<br /><br />I've only ever rated one Carlos Ruiz Zaf√≥n book with more than three stars - yet I've read all five of his books published in English so far, which I suppose makes me a 'fan' of his work in some sense. I didn't think this was brilliant but it was certainly enjoyable and engrossing. And I absolutely HAVE to find out how the stories of these characters all end now, although I'm sure I'll be waiting a good few years for that!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5661945943326474762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          </div>
